HTree — Grokipedia Search ⌘K Suggest Edit Sign in Sign in Introduction Definition and Purpose Key Characteristics History and Development Origins Evolution and Milestones Structure and Design Node Organization Hashing and Indexing Algorithms Insertion Process Search and Retrieval Applications Cache Optimization Variants and Extensions HTree in ext4 Performance and Analysis Advantages Limitations and Comparisons Implementation Considerations Practical Examples Challenges in Use References Fact-checked by Grok 5 months ago HTree Ara Eve Leo Sal 1x An HTree is a hashed tree data structure optimized for indexing directory entries in the ext2 and ext3 Linux file systems, enabling fast lookups in large directories by organizing entries based on filename hashes rather than linear scanning. [1] Introduced in the Linux kernel version 2.6, the HTree addresses the performance limitations of traditional ext2/ext3 directories, which store entries in a linear, linked-list-like format within fixed-size blocks (typically 4 KB), leading to O(n) search times that degrade severely for directories with thousands of files, such as those in web caches or mail systems. [1] Unlike more complex balanced trees like B-trees used in file systems such as ReiserFS or XFS, the HTree maintains a simple, constant-depth design (one or two levels) to align with ext2/ext3's emphasis on robustness and minimal complexity, while achieving 50–100x faster lookups for large directories. [1] The structure of an HTree begins with a 32-bit hash of each filename, computed using a filesystem-specific secret to enhance security against hash collision attacks, which serves as the key for indexing. Leaf nodes consist of standard directory blocks identical to non-HTree formats, ensuring backward compatibility, while internal index nodes are compact (8 bytes each), allowing a high fanout of over 500 child blocks per 4 KB index block and supporting up to 16 million entries without rebalancing. [1] The tree is flagged in the inode with the EXT3_INDEX_FL attribute, and index blocks are formatted to appear as deleted entries to older kernels, preventing misinterpretation. [1] In case of corruption in index nodes, the system gracefully falls back to linear scanning of leaf blocks, limiting data loss compared to more brittle tree implementations. [1] Key advantages include significant scalability for high-file-count directories, where linear structures become impractical, and forward/backward compatibility that allows seamless reading by legacy kernels without conversion. [1] However, operations like readdir() return entries in hash order rather than inode or alphabetical order, which can lead to inefficient disk access patterns for full-directory scans; this can be mitigated by application-level sorting or hash-based inode allocation strategies. [1] The HTree was developed by contributors including Daniel Phillips and Theodore Ts'o, with its implementation comprising around 5,000 lines of code integrated into the mainline kernel, and it remains a foundational feature in ext4 as well. [1] Introduction Definition and Purpose The HTree is a hashed tree data structure optimized for indexing directory entries in the ext2, ext3, and ext4 Linux file systems, enabling fast lookups in large directories by organizing entries based on filename hashes rather than linear scanning. [1] Introduced in the Linux kernel version 2.6, the HTree addresses the performance limitations of traditional ext2/ext3 directories, which store entries in a linear, linked-list-like format within fixed-size blocks (typically 4 KB), leading to O(n) search times that degrade severely for directories with thousands of files, such as those in web caches or mail systems. [1] Unlike more complex balanced trees like B-trees used in file systems such as ReiserFS or XFS, the HTree maintains a simple, constant-depth design (one or two levels, extendable to three in ext4 with the large_dir feature) to align with ext2/ext3's emphasis on robustness and minimal complexity, while achieving 50–100x faster lookups for large directories. [1] [2] Developed by Daniel Phillips in 2000 and integrated into the mainline kernel by Theodore Ts'o during the 2.5 development cycle, it was first available in kernel 2.5.40 and remains a core feature in ext4 as of Linux 6.x. [3] The structure of an HTree begins with a 32-bit hash of each filename, computed using a filesystem-specific secret (with versions supporting Half MD4, Tea, or Siphash) to enhance security against hash collision attacks, which serves as the key for indexing. Leaf nodes consist of standard directory blocks identical to non-HTree formats, ensuring backward compatibility, while internal index nodes are compact (8 bytes each), allowing a high fanout of over 500 child blocks per 4 KB index block and supporting up to 16 million entries without rebalancing. [1] [2] The tree is flagged in the inode with the EXT4_INDEX_FL attribute, and index blocks are formatted to appear as deleted entries to older kernels, preventing misinterpretation. [1] In case of corruption in index nodes, the system gracefully falls back to linear scanning of leaf blocks, limiting data loss compared to more brittle tree implementations. In ext4, metadata checksums further enhance integrity. [1] [2] Key Characteristics The HTree maintains a balanced tree property through its uniform-depth design, where all leaves reside at the same level, guaranteeing constant-depth access paths irrespective of insertion order. This balance is inherent to its hash-keyed structure, with each level partitioning the 32-bit hash key space to effectively halve the search domain, enabling a high branching factor (e.g., up to 503 for 4 KiB blocks) and typically limiting depth to one or two levels even for directories containing tens of millions of entries. As described by Phillips, "The main difference between an HTree and a BTree is that the leaves of an HTrees are all at the same depth," which eliminates the need for special leaf markers and dynamic adjustments while preserving O(1) or O(log n) performance with a large base. [3] A defining trait of the HTree is its cache-friendly architecture, optimized for modern memory hierarchies by minimizing block accesses and leveraging block-level granularity. Index nodes are structured to align with cache line sizes, such as 64 bytes, to exploit spatial locality during traversals and temporal locality across repeated operations on the same directory; typically, only two blocks (root and leaf) or three (for very large directories) are loaded into cache per lookup. Phillips emphasizes that "Because the index tree consists of a very small number of blocks, even for large directories, it is a practical certainty that they will all be retained in cache across multiple operations on the same directory," contrasting sharply with linear scans or deeper trees that incur frequent misses. [3] The HTree uses filename hashes as keys, transforming directory searches into efficient tree traversals while preserving compatibility. Hash collisions are handled by flagging and scanning adjacent leaf blocks, with rare splits occurring when blocks fill. In ext4 extensions, support for larger directories (up to three levels via INCOMPAT_LARGEDIR since Linux 4.13) and checksums (covering inode, entries, and tail structures) address scalability and reliability for modern workloads. Qian et al.'s work on chained hashes is unrelated to this filesystem structure. [3] [2] At the leaves, the HTree incorporates a bucket mechanism to optimize storage and access, with each leaf block serving as a fixed-size bucket holding 4-8 directory entries (or a range thereof) to minimize pointer overhead and enable compact representation. Index entries point to entire buckets rather than individual keys, allowing linear scans within the bucket for final resolution; collisions are flagged in the hash and resolved by checking adjacent buckets. Per Phillips, "Each hash key references not an individual directory entry, but a range of entries that are stored within a single leaf block," which balances density and lookup speed while fitting filesystem block constraints. [3] Unlike self-balancing trees such as AVL or red-black variants, the HTree requires no rebalancing post-construction, relying on its static, uniform-depth layout to sustain performance without rotations or splits beyond initial block partitioning. This eliminates runtime overhead from imbalance detection and correction, making it ideal for read-heavy workloads like directory indexing. Phillips underscores that "rebalancing is unnecessary, saving considerable complexity," as the design inherently avoids the imbalances plaguing dynamic trees after insertions or deletions. [3] History and Development Origins The HTree data structure was developed by Daniel Phillips in early 2001 as a solution to scalability issues in directory management within Unix-like file systems. Initially prototyped for the Ext2 filesystem in the Linux kernel, it addressed the limitations of traditional linear directory scans, which became prohibitively slow for directories containing thousands of files, leading to linear performance degradation (O(n) time complexity) during lookups, insertions, and deletions. Phillips designed the HTree to provide consistent, logarithmic-time operations regardless of file ordering or directory size, making it suitable for environments with growing data volumes. [4] The primary motivation stemmed from the increasing demands of large-scale file systems, where classic Ext2 directories relied on sequential block scans, causing uneven cache access patterns and significant slowdowns beyond 10,000 files—such as creation times ballooning from milliseconds to seconds. By organizing directory entries in a hashed, bounded-depth tree structure, HTree mitigated these issues, ensuring balanced access and enabling efficient handling of up to millions of entries per directory. This innovation was particularly relevant amid the rapid expansion of storage capacities in the early 2000s, where file system performance bottlenecks hindered overall system efficiency. [4] HTree was first publicly detailed in Phillips' paper "A Directory Index for Ext2," presented at the 5th Annual Linux Showcase & Conference in November 2001. The work included benchmarks demonstrating HTree's superiority over linear Ext2 and competing structures like those in ReiserFS, with linear scaling even in worst-case scenarios. Integrated into the Linux kernel around version 2.5.40, it marked a key advancement in open-source file system design, influencing subsequent extensions in Ext3 and Ext4. [4] Evolution and Milestones The HTree indexing structure was formally introduced in 2001 through a seminal paper by Daniel Phillips, presented at the 5th Annual Linux Showcase & Conference, where it was detailed as a hash-based tree designed to efficiently manage large directories in the Ext2 filesystem by providing logarithmic-time lookups while maintaining compatibility with existing formats. [5] Following its proposal, HTree saw rapid open-source adoption within the Linux kernel. It was integrated into the ext3 journaling filesystem via patches submitted in late 2002, becoming available in kernel version 2.5.47 and later stable releases, enabling hashed indexing for directories to mitigate performance degradation in file servers and large-scale storage environments. [6] This feature carried forward into ext4, which began development in 2006 and was initially merged into the mainline kernel as ext4dev in December 2006 (kernel 2.6.19), and stabilized in kernel 2.6.28 in October 2008, solidifying HTree as a core component for handling directories with millions of entries in modern Linux distributions. [7] Performance evaluations underscored HTree's impact on directory operations. A 2005 analysis of ext3 highlighted that HTree delivered speedups of 50–100x or more for lookups in very large directories compared to linear scans, addressing the quadratic scaling issues of traditional Ext2 structures and proving essential for workloads like mail servers with extensive subdirectory hierarchies. [1] Earlier benchmarks from the initial implementation similarly demonstrated up to 145x improvements in creation and lookup times for directories exceeding one million files, with typical moderate-sized directories benefiting from 2–3x faster access due to reduced block traversals. [5] Structure and Design Node Organization The HTree is a fixed-depth tree structure designed for simplicity and compatibility within the ext2 and ext3 file systems. It uses a 32-bit hash of the filename, computed with a filesystem-specific secret to prevent collision attacks, as the indexing key. The tree consists of at most two levels: the root and optional index blocks pointing to leaf blocks. [1] Leaf nodes are standard ext2/ext3 directory blocks, storing filename entries in a linear format within 4 KB blocks. These blocks are identical to those in non-HTree directories, ensuring that legacy kernels can read them directly. Within a leaf block, entries for filenames hashing to the same value are stored sequentially, allowing linear scanning only within a small bucket for collision resolution. [1] Internal index nodes are compact structures fitting up to 509 entries in a 4 KB block (after accounting for header overhead). Each entry is 8 bytes: 3 bytes for logical block number, 3 bytes for physical block number, and 2 unused bytes. These nodes reference leaf blocks based on hash value ranges, providing a high fanout that supports directories with millions of entries without deeper levels. The root node resides in the inode's i_data array and can directly act as a leaf if small, or point to index blocks if larger. [1] The tree height is constant at one or two levels, guaranteeing O(1) lookup time for the tree traversal, with any additional cost from scanning small hash collision buckets in leaves. This design avoids the complexity of balancing seen in B-trees, prioritizing robustness over dynamic adjustments. [1] For compatibility, index blocks are formatted to appear as special "deleted" directory entries to older kernels, which ignore them and scan leaves linearly. The inode is flagged with EXT3_INDEX_FL to indicate HTree usage. In case of index corruption, the system falls back to scanning all leaf blocks, minimizing data loss. [1] Hashing and Indexing Filename hashing uses a modified ELF hash algorithm seeded with a per-filesystem secret, producing a 32-bit value. The upper 16 bits select the leaf block within an index node (or directly from root), while the lower bits determine position within the block. This distributes entries evenly across buckets, reducing collisions. Insertions append to the appropriate bucket, and if a bucket overflows, a new leaf block is allocated. No rebalancing is performed, relying on the hash for distribution. [1] This structure achieves 50–100x faster lookups for directories with thousands of files compared to linear scanning, while maintaining full backward and forward compatibility. [1] Algorithms Insertion Process Insertion in an HTree directory begins with computing a 32-bit hash of the filename using a filesystem-specific hash function (typically a modified ELF hash incorporating a secret value for security). This hash serves as the key for locating the appropriate leaf block. Starting from the root (which may be in the inode or a block), the algorithm traverses the fixed-depth tree (1 or 2 levels) by examining the index block's array of 8-byte entries, each consisting of a 32-bit hash prefix (representing the upper bound for the subtree) and a 4-byte block pointer. The entries are sorted by hash prefix, and traversal finds the entry where the search hash is less than or equal to the prefix but greater than the previous one's, following that block pointer to the next level or leaf. [1] At the leaf block, which is a standard ext2/ext3 directory block with variable-length entries, the new entry (inode number, entry length, name length, filename) is added if space allows, maintaining compatibility with linear formats. If the leaf overflows (typically after ~100-200 entries depending on name lengths), it splits: the entries are redistributed into two new leaf blocks based on their hash values, creating balanced hash ranges. A new index entry is added to the parent index block pointing to the second leaf. Due to the high fanout (over 500 children per 4 KB index block), index block overflows are rare and handled similarly by splitting into new index blocks if needed, though the fixed depth limits propagation. No global rebalancing occurs, ensuring simplicity. During modifications, the EXT3_INDEX_FL inode flag is temporarily cleared for forward compatibility with older kernels. [1] The traversal and leaf insertion are O(1) time due to fixed depth and constant block sizes, with linear scan of the leaf (O(block size)) for exact placement. Splits are infrequent, amortized O(1) per insertion, involving sorting or partitioning a single block's entries (O(b log b) for block size b). [1] Search and Retrieval Search (lookup) computes the 32-bit filename hash and traverses from the root by selecting the appropriate child block in each index node. At an index block, the sorted list of hash prefixes is scanned (linearly or via binary search in implementations) to find the entry whose range contains the search hash, then follows the block pointer. This reaches the leaf block in O(1) steps due to fixed depth. The leaf block is then linearly scanned for the exact filename match among entries with compatible hashes, resolving collisions. This achieves near-constant time lookups, vastly improving over linear directory scans. [1] For readdir() operations, which enumerate all entries, the HTree is traversed level by level to visit all leaf blocks in hash order, scanning each linearly; entries are returned in hash-sorted order, which may lead to non-sequential disk access but can be sorted by applications if needed. Range queries (e.g., by name prefix) are not natively optimized but fall back to scanning relevant leaf blocks identified via partial hash matches. [1] Deletion follows the search path to the leaf, removes the entry, and if the leaf underflows significantly, may merge with a sibling, updating the parent index. Merges are rare due to the design's robustness. If index corruption is detected, the system falls back to linear scanning of all leaf blocks. [1] Applications Cache Optimization The HTree data structure is engineered to enhance processor cache efficiency, particularly for workloads involving frequent directory lookups and modifications. Its nodes are designed to span exactly one filesystem block, typically 4 KB on modern systems, which aligns with common cache line sizes (e.g., 64 bytes on x86 architectures) to minimize cache pollution and unnecessary line evictions during traversal. This alignment ensures that accessing an index node loads a complete, self-contained unit into the cache without partial line fetches or fragmentation overhead. [5] A key locality benefit arises from the HTree's routing mechanism, where sibling nodes—representing adjacent hash ranges—are stored contiguously within the same index block. This spatial proximity leverages the hypercube-inspired layout of hash partitioning, keeping related data nearby in memory and promoting prefetching by the cache hierarchy. During search operations, the binary search on sorted index entries within a block exploits temporal locality, as the root and intermediate blocks often remain resident in L1 or L2 caches across sequential directory accesses. Studies of HTree implementations in file systems demonstrate improved hit rates, with reduced random memory accesses compared to linear scanning in unindexed directories. [5] In practice, HTree's performance in directory operations benefits from these optimizations, providing O(log n) lookups with up to 145x speedup over linear scanning in benchmarks of large directories (e.g., 10,000+ files). For instance, the compact index structure (typically 1-3 blocks) ensures high cache retention for repeated accesses, mitigating thrashing in memory-constrained environments. This design is deployed in the ext4 filesystem, where it supports scalable directory operations for large-scale applications like web servers and package repositories, handling millions of entries efficiently since Linux kernel 2.6.27. [5] [8] Variants and Extensions HTree in ext4 The HTree directory indexing feature introduced in ext3 was carried over and enhanced in the ext4 filesystem. In ext4, directories using HTree can support an effectively unlimited number of subdirectories, removing the 32,000 limit present in ext3. This is achieved through the use of extents for the index blocks, allowing more efficient allocation and reducing fragmentation in large directories. The dir_index feature flag remains the same, ensuring compatibility with ext3. As of Linux kernel 2.6.28, ext4 provides backward compatibility for HTree-indexed directories when mounted as ext3. Additionally, ext4 introduces checksums for directory entries and index blocks to detect and handle corruption more robustly, building on the HTree's fallback to linear scanning in case of index corruption. These improvements make HTree more reliable for modern workloads with millions of files per directory. [8] Performance and Analysis Advantages HTrees offer significant simplicity in design and implementation compared to self-balancing trees like B-trees, as they eliminate the need for dynamic rebalancing algorithms, which can introduce substantial complexity, especially when handling hash key collisions or additional requirements of variants such as B+-trees and B*-trees. [3] This results in a structure that can be implemented in just a few hundred lines of code, aligning with the minimalist philosophy of filesystems like Ext2, while avoiding the potential to double the codebase size required for full B-tree support. [3] The fixed height and uniform depth of HTrees ensure predictable performance, with all leaves at the same level, enabling consistent access latencies and efficient caching of index blocks across operations. [3] Typically, lookups require accessing only two or three blocks (an index block and a leaf, or an additional level for very large directories), providing O(log n) time complexity for individual operations and avoiding the O(n) degradation of linear searches in traditional structures. [3] HTrees demonstrate excellent scalability, supporting directories with up to 75,000 entries using a single index block, over 30 million with two levels, and theoretically over 11 billion with three levels, without performance degradation. [3] This high branching factor—up to 508 leaf blocks per 4KB index block—keeps the tree shallow, making it suitable for enterprise-scale scenarios previously limited by directory size constraints in unindexed filesystems. [3] Empirical benchmarks highlight HTree's efficiency, showing a 145-fold improvement in directory operations over standard Ext2 for large directories in controlled tests, while maintaining performance comparable to or better than traditional methods for smaller ones. [3] In the implemented version in Linux 2.6 ext3, lookups in large directories achieve 50–100 times faster performance compared to linear scanning. [1] In cache-constrained environments, this persistent indexing avoids the cold-cache penalties and thrashing risks associated with on-the-fly hash tables, leading to more reliable lookup times. [3] HTrees incur low overhead through minimal metadata: each index entry consists of just 8 bytes (a 32-bit hash key and a block pointer), with a compact 32-byte header per block, resulting in an index size less than half that of the leaf blocks while achieving approximately 75% average fullness. [3] Compared to B-trees, which require more index blocks (one entry per directory entry rather than per leaf block), HTrees use far fewer resources and maintain compatibility with existing filesystem formats via a simple inode flag. [3] Limitations and Comparisons While HTrees provide efficient indexing for directory lookups in file systems like ext3 and ext4, they exhibit drawbacks in handling highly skewed data distributions. Nonuniform hash functions can lead to uneven splitting of the hash key space, resulting in average leaf fullness as low as 50% in worst-case scenarios with nonrandom input strings, compared to an ideal 75% for uniform distributions. [9] This fragmentation risk increases space overhead, as coalescing mechanisms remain unimplemented and underfilled blocks accumulate free space. Additionally, splits during insertions require sorting entries by hash value, incurring O(n log n) time complexity per affected block, though such operations are rare (less than 1 per 100 creations for 4KB blocks). [9] In comparison to B-trees, HTrees offer advantages in cache performance due to their uniform shallow depth (typically 1-2 levels) and high branching factor, avoiding the rebalancing overhead that can deepen B-tree structures and degrade average search times. [9] B-trees also support ordered traversals and dynamic balancing for insertions/deletions, making them more versatile for general database indexing, whereas HTrees are optimized specifically for point queries in linear data like file names. For full directory scans, such as readdir() operations, HTrees can lead to inefficient disk access patterns due to entries being returned in hash order rather than inode or alphabetical order, potentially causing inode table thrashing in large directories; this was noted in the Linux 2.6 ext3 implementation and can be mitigated by application-level sorting. [1] In case of index corruption, HTrees gracefully fall back to linear scanning of leaf blocks, preserving data accessibility unlike more complex tree structures. [1] Implementation Considerations Practical Examples The HTree implementation in the Linux kernel's ext4 filesystem is written in C and uses specific structures defined in include/linux/ext4_dir.h . Key structures include struct dx_root for the root node, struct dx_node for internal nodes, and struct dx_entry for hash-to-block mappings. Each dx_entry is 8 bytes: a 32-bit hash value and a 32-bit block pointer. The hash is computed using one of several algorithms (legacy, half MD4, or TEA), selected via the hash_version field in struct dx_root_info . [2] The root node occupies the first data block of the directory inode, starting with standard "." and ".." entries, followed by metadata and an array of dx_entry structures pointing to leaf or internal blocks. Internal nodes format the entire block as a fake deleted directory entry (inode=0) to ensure compatibility with older kernels, containing similar metadata and dx_entry arrays. Leaf nodes are standard ext4 directory blocks with struct ext4_dir_entry_2 entries, optionally extended for features like encryption, and may include checksums via struct ext4_dir_entry_tail . The tree depth is specified by indirect_levels in the root info (up to 3 levels with the INCOMPAT_LARGEDIR feature). [2] Lookups begin by computing a 32-bit hash of the filename and traversing from the root: select the appropriate dx_entry based on hash range, follow the block pointer, and repeat until reaching a leaf block, where entries are scanned linearly for an exact match (handling collisions). If the index flag is not set or corruption is detected, the system falls back to linear scanning of all blocks. Source code for these operations is in fs/ext4/namei.c , including functions like ext4_htree_store_dirent for insertions and ext4_dx_find_entry for lookups. [2] Open-source implementations outside the kernel are rare, as HTree is tailored to ext4's block-based design. For reference, the kernel source provides a robust example of disk-based tree management with compatibility features. Configuration involves enabling the index flag during directory creation (e.g., via mkdir on an ext4 filesystem with dir_index feature), and tools like debugfs can inspect structures with the htree command. Challenges in Use Implementing HTrees effectively requires addressing several practical hurdles that can impact performance, reliability, and maintainability. One key challenge is handling skew caused by uneven distribution of hash values, which can lead to imbalanced trees, reduced search efficiency, and increased fragmentation. Nonuniform hash functions, particularly those performing poorly on nonrandom inputs like sequentially numbered filenames, cluster entries and lower average leaf node fullness to as low as 50% in practice. To mitigate this, preprocessing strategies such as salting the hash with a random value are employed; in the ext4 filesystem's HTree, a per-directory random parameter is stored in the root block and incorporated into the hash calculation, promoting uniform distribution and thwarting attacks that exploit predictable collisions. [3] Memory fragmentation arises in large HTrees due to dynamic allocation of nodes and blocks, resulting in inefficient use of storage and potential performance degradation from scattered accesses. In disk-based implementations like ext4, uneven splitting from hash skew exacerbates on-disk fragmentation, with theoretical 75% leaf fullness dropping significantly under real-world conditions, necessitating more blocks for the same number of entries. While ext4 mitigates general file fragmentation through extents, tree node allocation in custom or user-space HTree implementations can suffer from heap fragmentation; solutions like memory arenas—pre-allocating contiguous blocks for node storage—help by minimizing overhead and external fragmentation in dynamic growth scenarios. [3] [10] Concurrency control poses difficulties in multi-threaded or multi-process environments, where simultaneous reads and writes risk data corruption or inconsistent views. HTrees demand fine-grained locking to support high throughput; reader-writer locks are often used, permitting multiple concurrent readers while serializing writers to maintain balance during splits or insertions. In the Linux kernel's ext4 HTree, directory operations leverage the inode's i_rwsem for this purpose, allowing parallel lookups via shared locks but exclusive access for modifications like htree_split_leaf , ensuring atomicity without excessive contention. Portability challenges emerge when adapting HTrees across architectures, particularly with varying endianness or key sizes. Bit masks and hash computations must account for byte order differences, as mismatched handling can corrupt node pointers or keys. Ext4 structures, including HTree nodes, use little-endian encodings (e.g., __le32 for hash values), with the kernel automatically swapping bytes on big-endian systems like PowerPC to ensure cross-platform compatibility; for non-64-bit keys, masks are adjusted to prevent overflow, though this may require custom hash functions to preserve uniformity. [11] Debugging HTrees often focuses on verifying balance and integrity post-insertion, as imbalances or corruption can silently degrade performance. Tools for visualization include the debugfs utility in Linux, whose htree command dumps the internal structure of an indexed directory, revealing levels, hash ranges per node, entry counts, and potential skew indicators like uneven subtree sizes. This allows developers to inspect post-insertion balance, identify hot spots from poor hashing, and trigger repairs via e2fsck if integrity checks in the root header fail. [12] References https://www.kernel.org/doc/ols/2005/ols2005v1-pages-77-104.pdf https://www.kernel.org/doc/html/latest/filesystems/ext4/directory.html https://www.kernel.org/doc/ols/2002/ols2002-pages-425-438.pdf https://lwn.net/2001/1213/a/directory-index.php3 https://www.usenix.org/legacy/publications/library/proceedings/als01/full_papers/phillips/phillips.pdf https://lwn.net/Articles/11481/ https://kernel.org/doc/ols/2007/ols2007v2-pages-21-34.pdf https://www.kernel.org/doc/html/latest/filesystems/ext4.html https://www.usenix.org/legacy/publications/library/proceedings/als01/full_papers/phillips/phillips_html/ https://pages.cs.wisc.edu/~madhurm/ext4FileSystem.pdf https://blogs.oracle.com/linux/understanding-ext4-disk-layout-part-2 https://blogs.oracle.com/linux/directory-entry-lookup-in-ext4 Table of Contents Introduction Definition and Purpose Key Characteristics History and Development Origins Evolution and Milestones Structure and Design Node Organization Hashing and Indexing Algorithms Insertion Process Search and Retrieval Applications Cache Optimization Variants and Extensions HTree in ext4 Performance and Analysis Advantages Limitations and Comparisons Implementation Considerations Practical Examples Challenges in Use References Sign in to contribute Create an account or sign in to suggest articles and edits to Grokipedia. From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia Tree data structure for directory indexing Not to be confused with H tree, a family of fractal sets. This article relies excessively on references to primary sources. Please improve this article by adding secondary or tertiary sources. Find sources: "HTree" – news · newspapers · books · scholar · JSTOR ( September 2013) ( Learn how and when to remove this message) An HTree is a specialized tree data structure for directory indexing, similar to a B-tree. They are constant depth of either one or two levels, have a high fanout factor, use a hash of the filename, and do not require balancing. [1] The HTree algorithm is distinguished from standard B-tree methods by its treatment of hash collisions, which may overflow across multiple leaf and index blocks. HTree indexes are used in the ext3 and ext4 Linux filesystems, and were incorporated into the Linux kernel around 2.5.40. [2] HTree indexing improved the scalability of Linux ext2 based filesystems from a practical limit of a few thousand files, into the range of tens of millions of files per directory. History [ edit] The HTree index data structure and algorithm were developed by Daniel Phillips in 2000 and implemented for the ext2 filesystem in February 2001. A port to the ext3 filesystem by Christopher Li and Andrew Morton in 2002 during the 2.5 kernel series added journal based crash consistency. With minor improvements, HTree continues to be used in ext4 in the Linux 3.x.x kernel series. Use [ edit] ext2 HTree indexes were originally developed for ext2 but the patch never made it to the official branch. The dir_index feature can be enabled when creating an ext2 filesystem, but the ext2 code won't act on it. ext3 HTree indexes are available in ext3 when the dir_index feature is enabled. ext4 HTree indexes are turned on by default in ext4. This feature is implemented in Linux kernel 2.6.23. HTree indexes is also used for file extents when a file needs more than the 4 extents stored in the inode. The large_dir feature of ext4 is implemented in Linux kernel 4.13. PHTree [ edit] PHTree (Physically stable HTree) is a derivation intended as a successor. [3] [unreliablesource?] It fixes all the known issues with HTree except for write multiplication. [citationneeded] It is used in the Tux3 filesystem. [4] References [ edit] ^ Mingming Cao. "Directory indexing" (PDF). Features found in Linux 2.6. ^ tytso@mit.edu. "Add ext3 indexed directory (htree) support". ^ "PHTree design refresh". 4 January 2013. ^ "Tux3 Versioning Filesystem". Archived from the original on 13 January 2015. Retrieved 28 December 2014. HTree Indexed Directory Ask Question Asked 6 years, 4 months ago Modified 2 years, 2 months ago Viewed 2k times This question shows research effort; it is useful and clear 2 This question does not show any research effort; it is unclear or not useful Save this question. Show activity on this post. Reading the man page of chattr , I came across the I flag for Indexed Directory . Upon investigation it turned out that this refers to HTree indexed directories, as described by this paper. It says that hashed tree provided a similar performance to BTrees, but are way simpler to implement. After running lsattr in the home on my Ubuntu machine, I noticed the the Downloads directory has the Indexed Directory flag set, but nothing else has. I also noticed, that stat --format "%s" Downloads tells me its size was 12 KiB instead of the 4 KiB I got for all other directories in my home. I searched the Internet for further information on this topic, but only got this paper from 2001. The system is an Ubuntu 19.10 with kernel 5.3.0-26 on an ext4 root. My questions are: What's the practical difference between HTree and non-HTree directories? How can I create them? How was it created? Why isn't any directory a HTree one? linux filesystems ext4 Share Short permalink to this question Improve this question Follow Follow this question to receive notifications asked Jan 27, 2020 at 10:28 bot47 1,328 4 4 gold badges 14 14 silver badges 24 24 bronze badges Add a comment | 2 Answers 2 Sorted by: Reset to default Highest score (default) Date modified (newest first) Date created (oldest first) This answer is useful 3 This answer is not useful Save this answer. Loading when this answer was accepted… Show activity on this post. In any modern ext3/ext4 filesystem, all directories larger than a single filesystem block (typically 4KB) will be indexed. This happens automatically when the directory grows beyond the first block. There isn't any particular way to "create" an htree directory, beyond adding more entries than can fit into one block (depends on filename length, maybe 60-100 files). Once a directory grows in size, it will never be shrunk by ext4, though there are some patches floating around that may implement this one day. Share Short permalink to this answer Improve this answer Follow Follow this answer to receive notifications answered Jan 28, 2020 at 2:41 LustreOne 2,081 10 10 silver badges 21 21 bronze badges 4 So why are there both implementations of directory indexing then? bot47 – bot47 2020-01-29 05:56:53 +00:00 Commented Jan 29, 2020 at 5:56 There is only one implementation of indexed directory. Non-htree directories are searched linearly. With new filesystems this should not normally happen, but older filesystems may have non-htree directories, or if there is an error detected it will clear the index flag and fall back to linear searching. LustreOne – LustreOne 2020-01-30 06:14:49 +00:00 Commented Jan 30, 2020 at 6:14 @LustreOne, would you share some source where I can find about this automatic indexing on directory growth? user2553863 – user2553863 2023-11-01 14:44:51 +00:00 Commented Nov 1, 2023 at 14:44 Note there isn't a "index" for looking up exact filenames, per-se, since entries are inserted into the directory leaf blocks without any ordering. There is an index to find the right leaf block by the hash of the filename, and then a linear search os done within the leaf block.The split of a single directory block into an htree is done in fs/ext4/namei.c::make_indexed_dir() . LustreOne – LustreOne 2023-11-05 12:35:59 +00:00 Commented Nov 5, 2023 at 12:35 Add a comment | This answer is useful -1 This answer is not useful Save this answer. Loading when this answer was accepted… Show activity on this post. Currently, after removing the immutable(i) attribute of the parent directory, subdirectories with the indexed directory(I) attribute can be deleted directly. Source: Implement B+ Tree in Go | Implement Data Structures in Programming Languages - SSOJet
URL: https://ssojet.com/data-structures/implement-b-plus-tree-in-go
Implement B+ Tree in Go | Implement Data Structures in Programming Languages [-] Why SSOJet Pricing Sign in Start 30-day free trial Sign in Start 30-day free trial Table of contents B+ Tree Structure and Node Design Core B+ Tree Operations: Insertion Core B+ Tree Operations: Search and Deletion Go-Specific Implementation Considerations Share Link Copied! Ready to make Implement Data Structures in Programming Languages a business advantage? Sign up today. Get started Explore with AI ChatGPT Read with ChatGPT Read with Claude Read with AI Mode Home Implement B+ Tree in Go Implement B+ Tree in Go July 18, 2025 4 min read When dealing with large datasets that need efficient searching, insertion, and deletion, a B+ tree is often the go-to data structure. This article walks you through implementing a B+ tree from scratch in Go. You'll learn how to manage nodes, handle splits and merges, and optimize read/write operations. By the end, you'll have a robust B+ tree implementation ready to integrate into your Go applications, significantly improving performance for data-intensive tasks. B+ Tree Structure and Node Design A B+ Tree is organized into nodes, each holding keys and either data pointers or child node pointers. We can represent this in Go with a struct like this: The key distinction lies between leaf and internal nodes. Leaf nodes contain the actual data (or pointers to it) and are linked sequentially. Internal nodes, conversely, only store keys that act as routing information to guide search operations down the tree; they don't hold data themselves. A critical design parameter is node capacity, often defined by maxKeys . This dictates how many keys (and associated entries) a node can hold. A common pitfall is hardcoding maxKeys without analyzing typical data sizes or anticipated query patterns. This oversight can lead to inefficient disk I/O, as nodes might be too small, causing excessive tree height, or too large, leading to wasted space and slower node traversals. Carefully tune maxKeys based on your specific workload for optimal performance. Core B+ Tree Operations: Insertion The Insert function handles adding new key-value pairs to the B+ Tree. It typically involves a recursive helper function that navigates to the correct leaf node. Once the key-value pair is added to the leaf, the function checks if the leaf has exceeded its capacity ( maxEntries ). If a leaf node splits, a new sibling leaf is created. Keys and values are redistributed, and the median key is promoted to the parent node. For example, if a leaf node with keys [10, 20, 30, 40] splits and maxEntries is 3, the original leaf might hold [10, 20] and the new sibling [30, 40] . The key 30 would be promoted to the parent. Root splits are special cases where the tree height increases. A common gotcha is failing to update parent pointers correctly after a split, which can corrupt the tree structure and break traversals. Always ensure parent pointers are meticulously managed during splits. Core B+ Tree Operations: Search and Deletion The Search function in a B+ tree efficiently locates a key by traversing from the root. At each internal node, keys are used to determine which child pointer to follow, guiding the search down until the appropriate leaf node is reached. For instance, to find key 42 , you'd start at the root, compare 42 with the node's keys, and descend to the child pointer that encompasses 42 's range. This process repeats until the leaf node containing 42 is found. Delete operations require careful handling. After removing a key from a leaf node, an underflow check is performed. If a node has too few keys (below the minimum threshold), it attempts to borrow a key from an adjacent sibling or merges with a sibling. This redistribution or merging process might propagate upwards, potentially affecting parent nodes. A common gotcha is deleting a key in an internal node that serves only as a separator; this often necessitates replacing it with the minimum key from its right child or merging nodes. Always ensure parent pointers are accurately updated after any structural changes. Go-Specific Implementation Considerations When implementing a B+ tree in Go, leverage the language's built-in slice manipulation for efficient key and value management within nodes. For instance, append is ideal for adding new keys and values, while slice indexing and copy are useful for redistributing elements during node splits or merges. Be mindful of concurrency if your tree needs to handle simultaneous access. Employing sync.RWMutex at the node level or globally is crucial to prevent race conditions during concurrent reads and writes. A common gotcha is the performance hit from frequent slice reallocations when using append naively. Pre-allocating slices with a known capacity, like make([]KeyType, 0, maxKeys) , significantly mitigates this overhead. Optimize by pre-allocating or using memory pooling for demanding applications. Source: Limits of files you can store in a directory - Zhi Rui Tam
URL: https://zrt.wtf/what-limits-you-from-storing-inifinite-files/
Limits of files you can store in a directory - theblackcat102 theblackcat102 Publications Talks Blog Posts CV Zhi Rui Tam male / home sapiens ( ´ ω ` ) Follow Taiwan Appier Google Scholar Github X (formerly Twitter) Limits of files you can store in a directory 3 minute read Published: November 27, 2021 Its normal for a typical research projects to store each data as an individual file under a directory. Although this is not idea, no one seems to question what are the limits of such method. Ext4 Ext4 is the most commonly used format in linux environment nowadays so we will focus in finding the limit of files under a directory. According to this stackoverflow answer, ext4 limits is 2^32-1 (inode table maximum size) likely due to compatibility with 32-bit filesystem. So the answer is 2^32 -1 or 4 billions right? Introducing Index node aka Inode However under linux things doesn't work this way. In order to efficiently access each files, they are stored as index node or we typically call inodes. With this unique index number, we can do symbolic link which points different files to the same inode id. Inode is also where you store the permissions, owner, group settings. Now the bad news is inodes are limited, but they usually comes with in a very large number. This default value grows proportionally with disk size. For example here's a list of inodes number I get in my machine using df -i . As you can see filling both of my SSDs almost full with hundreds of millions of files doesn't even come close to reaching the inode limit. Unless you are storing billions of very small files ( ~ 1kB) inodes won't be a problem. If you are the 1% who have such problems, I would recommend you take a look into this thread to create filesystem with larger inode size. So everything is fine right? Say hello to hash collision This is practically CS data structure 101, so I would dwell into what hash collision is. Since a single directory can potentially hold up to 2^32 files, ext4 also maintains a hash table of size 2^32 to speed up filename search. This translate to subdirectory limits as well right? dir_nlink As mentioned in manpage about ext4 Normally, ext4 allows an inode to have no more than 65,000 hard links. This applies to regular files as well as directories, which means that there can be no more than 64,998 subdirectories in a directory So no, it doesn't Issues with ext4 high inode setup Although setting to the upper limits of ext4 inode count seems to solve most of other stackoverflow problems, this doesn't solve mine. Out of space errors still occur after reaching 15 millions of unique files in the filesystem. Using df -i shows inode counts using only 4% of total inodes. Hence I believe something is still missing which I failed to considered. I decided to use XFS instead for solving the problem once and for all, since it has built in dynamic inodes structure by sacrificing more storage space ( empty xfs filesystem uses about 15G space in a 8T hard disk ). So far I was able to store all 30M files without hurdles at all. Reading materials Ext4 high level design documentation Simple filesystem introduction if you don't have time to read 1 ext4: Mysterious “No space left on device”-errors Share on Twitter Facebook LinkedIn Previous Next You May Also Enjoy What Chess (Might) Taught Us About Programming with AI 14 minute read Published: June 24, 2025 The rapid evolution of AI coding assistants has sparked intense debate about the future of software engineering. Will AI replace programmers? Are we witnessing the end of coding as we know it? Recent research ( Lee et al., 2025; Kosmyna et al., 2025) suggests AI tools can reduce critical thinking and create cognitive dependencies. But I think the chess story from decades ago offers a more nuanced perspective. Rather than replacement, we might be witnessing transformation. GPU乞丐從 NeurIPS 2024 看2025年 LLM 題目趨勢 2 minute read Published: January 04, 2025 先說以下純屬我個人能逛到 poster 的部分來評論，因為場地太大、人群恐懼我沒辦法全部 poster 都看到。 Short writeup of “Unpopular opinions on AI” 9 minute read Published: June 22, 2023 “Unpopular opinions on AI” is a podcast episode by ZhenFund team thoughts on LLM at a sharing in China in simplfied chinese with Peak Ji Short writeup of recent challenges found in LLMs models (May 2023) 5 minute read Published: May 27, 2023 TLDR bullet points by GPT-4, so you don't need to wait for the plugins to generate it for you. Source: Master File Table (MFT), NTFS, $LogFile, and $UsnJrnl: Forensics | DFIR-Notes
URL: https://mahmoud-shaker.gitbook.io/dfir-notes/master-file-table-mft-ntfs-usdlogfile-and-usdusnjrnl-forensics
Master File Table (MFT), NTFS, $LogFile, and $UsnJrnl: Forensics | DFIR-Notes DFIR-Notes ⌘ Ctrl k For the complete documentation index, see llms.txt. This page is also available as Markdown. Copy On this page Master File Table (MFT), NTFS, $LogFile, and $UsnJrnl: Forensics Introduction The Master File Table (MFT), New Technology File System (NTFS), $LogFile, and $UsnJrnl are critical components of the NTFS file system. These structures serve as the backbone of modern file systems and are invaluable in the field of digital forensics. Their intricate design allows forensic investigators to reconstruct activity timelines, detect malicious behavior, and uncover hidden evidence.
Master File Table (MFT) Overview The Master File Table (MFT) is the central database in the NTFS file system, containing metadata about every file and directory. Each file or directory has a corresponding record in the MFT, including system files. Structure Each MFT entry consists of: File Reference Number: Unique identifier for each file. Standard Information Attribute: Contains timestamps such as creation, modification, access, and entry modification. File Name Attribute: Stores the name and parent directory information. Data Attribute: Points to the location of the file's content on disk (either resident or non-resident). Other Attributes: Security descriptors, object IDs, and more. Forensic Benefits File Activity Timeline: MFT provides creation, modification, and access timestamps, crucial for reconstructing timelines. Deleted File Recovery: Metadata in the MFT remains even if files are deleted, enabling recovery. Correlation with Other Artifacts: MFT data can corroborate findings from other NTFS structures like $LogFile and $UsnJrnl. Tools for Analysis MFTECmd: Parses MFT records for detailed analysis. Autopsy/Sleuth Kit: Includes modules to extract and analyze MFT data. FTK Imager: Allows raw access to the MFT.
New Technology File System (NTFS) Overview NTFS is a journaling file system introduced by Microsoft to provide improved reliability, security, and performance over previous file systems like FAT32. It supports advanced features such as encryption, compression, and large file sizes. Key Features Journaling: Tracks file system changes to ensure data integrity after crashes. Permissions and Security: Integrates with Access Control Lists (ACLs). File Compression: Saves disk space without affecting access speed. Sparse Files: Optimizes storage by only allocating disk space for non-zero data. Forensic Benefits Comprehensive Metadata: NTFS structures, including the MFT and $LogFile, provide a wealth of forensic data. File Recovery: Journaling and metadata aid in recovering corrupted or deleted files. Activity Analysis: Correlation of multiple NTFS artifacts helps identify malicious or unauthorized activity. Tools for Analysis X-Ways Forensics: Provides detailed NTFS analysis. AccessData FTK: Comprehensive file system investigation capabilities. EnCase: Offers robust NTFS parsing and artifact extraction.
$LogFile Overview The $LogFile is a transactional log that records changes to the NTFS volume. It ensures data consistency in the event of a system crash by replaying or rolling back transactions during recovery. Structure Metadata Records: Logs changes to MFT entries, directory structures, and volume metadata. Redo/Undo Records: Contains instructions for replaying or undoing changes. Forensic Benefits Granular Changes: Tracks detailed file and directory modifications. Failed Operations: Logs incomplete transactions, revealing potential tampering. Historical Insights: Provides insights into past activity, even if logs are partially overwritten. Tools for Analysis LogFileParser: Extracts and decodes $LogFile records. X-Ways Forensics: Parses $LogFile to identify system changes. WinHex: Allows manual inspection of $LogFile contents
Update Sequence Number (USN) Journal ($UsnJrnl) Overview The $UsnJrnl tracks changes to files and directories on NTFS volumes. It's enabled by default on modern Windows systems to support features like File History and indexing. Structure Change Journal Metadata: Stores volume and journal settings. Records: Each record contains information about the file, the type of change (create, delete, rename, etc.), and timestamps. Forensic Benefits Change Tracking: Logs file system events, revealing activity even if traditional logs are unavailable. File Deletion Evidence: Tracks deletions, renames, and attribute changes. Timeline Reconstruction: Provides precise timestamps for file system events. ExtendUsnJrnl: Tracks file and directory changes on an NTFS volume. $Max: Contains metadata about the USN Journal configuration. $J: Contains records of filesystem operations. We can see the two files in our Cases folder Tools for Analysis UsnJrnl2Csv: Converts USN Journal records to a readable format. RECmd: Extracts and analyzes $UsnJrnl data. X-Ways Forensics: Includes functionality to parse $UsnJrnl records. Summary The MFT, NTFS, $LogFile, and $UsnJrnl are integral components of the NTFS file system that serve operational and forensic purposes. They provide: Detailed metadata and event logs for forensic timelines. Evidence of file creation, deletion, and modification. Resilience against data loss due to journaling. By leveraging tools like MFTECmd, LogFileParser, and X-Ways Forensics, investigators can uncover hidden evidence, reconstruct events, and ensure the integrity of their findings. These artifacts are a cornerstone of digital forensics, making NTFS-based investigations robust and reliable.
