The TLB ¶ When the kernel unmaps or modified the attributes of a range of memory, it has two choices: Flush the entire TLB with a two-instruction sequence. This is a quick operation, but it causes collateral damage: TLB entries from areas other than the one we are trying to flush will be destroyed and must be refilled later, at some cost. Use the invlpg instruction to invalidate a single page at a time. This could potentially cost many more instructions, but it is a much more precise operation, causing no collateral damage to other TLB entries. Which method to do depends on a few things: The size of the flush being performed. A flush of the entire address space is obviously better performed by flushing the entire TLB than doing 2^48/PAGE_SIZE individual flushes. The contents of the TLB. If the TLB is empty, then there will be no collateral damage caused by doing the global flush, and all of the individual flush will have ended up being wasted work. The size of the TLB. The larger the TLB, the more collateral damage we do with a full flush. So, the larger the TLB, the more attractive an individual flush looks. Data and instructions have separate TLBs, as do different page sizes. The microarchitecture. The TLB has become a multi-level cache on modern CPUs, and the global flushes have become more expensive relative to single-page flushes. There is obviously no way the kernel can know all these things, especially the contents of the TLB during a given flush. The sizes of the flush will vary greatly depending on the workload as well. There is essentially no “right” point to choose. You may be doing too many individual invalidations if you see the invlpg instruction (or instructions near it) show up high in profiles. If you believe that individual invalidations being called too often, you can lower the tunable: This will cause us to do the global flush for more cases. Lowering it to 0 will disable the use of the individual flushes. Setting it to 1 is a very conservative setting and it should never need to be 0 under normal circumstances. Despite the fact that a single individual flush on x86 is guaranteed to flush a full 2MB [ 1], hugetlbfs always uses the full flushes. THP is treated exactly the same as normal memory. You might see invlpg inside of flush_tlb_mm_range() show up in profiles, or you can use the trace_tlb_flush() tracepoints. to determine how long the flush operations are taking. Essentially, you are balancing the cycles you spend doing invlpg with the cycles that you spend refilling the TLB later. You can measure how expensive TLB refills are by using performance counters and 'perf stat', like this: That works on an IvyBridge-era CPU (i5-3320M). Different CPUs may have differently-named counters, but they should at least be there in some form. You can use pmu-tools 'ocperf list' ( https://github.com/andikleen/pmu-tools) to find the right counters for a given CPU. [ 1] A footnote in Intel's SDM “4.10.4.2 Recommended Invalidation” says: “One execution of INVLPG is sufficient even for a page with size greater than 4 KBytes.” ©The kernel development community. | Powered by Sphinx 5.3.0 & Alabaster 0.7.16 | Page source

Source: Architectural Analysis of Virtual Memory Systems: Translation, Hierarchical Paging, and Multi-Core Coherence
Type: Pasted Text
Architectural Analysis of Virtual Memory Systems: Translation, Hierarchical Paging, and Multi-Core CoherenceVirtual Memory Foundations and Address Space AbstractionOperating systems enforce process isolation, security, and efficient memory utilization by virtualizing physical RAM into abstract address spaces.[1, 2] In early computing systems, applications directly manipulated physical memory or relied on static relocation, a software-based approach where a loader rewrote instruction operands to match physical offsets.[3, 4] However, static relocation lacked hardware-enforced protection, meaning any errant instruction could corrupt the operating system kernel or overwrite concurrent applications.[4] Later iterations attempted to implement time-sharing by swapping a process's entire memory footprint to disk during context switches, but this introduced severe I/O overhead.[1, 3] To enable low-latency context switching, modern operating systems maintain multiple resident processes in physical memory concurrently, requiring a hardware-enforced isolation framework.[1, 3]The address space abstraction represents a running program's private view of memory.[1, 3] Within this virtual address space, the layout is logically structured into three primary regions: program code containing static instructions at the top; a dynamically allocated, user-managed heap growing downward; and a stack growing upward from the bottom to store local variables and execution frames.[1]To virtualize this structure, the operating system and underlying hardware must satisfy three architectural goals [1, 3, 5]:Transparency: A program must execute under the illusion that it has exclusive access to a continuous, private physical memory pool, completely unaware of physical mapping configurations.[1, 3, 5]Efficiency: The hardware-software translation subsystem must minimize execution time and spatial memory overhead.[1, 3]Protection: The memory subsystem must isolate concurrent processes, preventing user-space applications from reading or overwriting the physical memory of other processes or the kernel.[1, 2, 3]In modern systems, the virtual memory framework is heavily pushed by data-intensive workloads, such as large language models (LLMs) and distributed database pipelines.[1, 2] For example, modern LLM text generation relies on attention mechanisms that maintain massive Key-Value (KV) cache representations of prior tokens to avoid redundant context computation.[1] Because conventional system RAM cannot deliver the required memory bandwidth, architectures have adopted High Bandwidth Memory (HBM) stacks placed adjacent to compute cores.[1]However, adjacent HBM is capacity-constrained.[1] This capacity ceiling has driven the adoption of tiered memory topologies, where the operating system dynamically migrates hot pages to adjacent HBM while spilling colder overflow pages to cheaper Compute Express Link (CXL)-attached system RAM.[1] Under such tiered arrangements, the performance and latency of the address translation mechanism determine the overall throughput of the system.[1, 2]
Address Translation Mechanics and Hardware ArchitectureAddress translation is the process of converting a program-generated virtual address (VA) into a physical address (PA) before executing a physical RAM access.[2, 5] The earliest hardware-assisted translation mechanisms utilized base-and-bounds registers.[3] During execution, the Memory Management Unit (MMU) added the value of a base register to the virtual address to yield the physical address, verifying that the result was within the limits of a bounds register.[3] While simple, this approach suffered from external fragmentation and inflexibility, requiring entire segments to remain contiguous in physical RAM.[3] When segments needed to grow, the operating system had to update the registers via system calls; failure to find contiguous space or attempts to access out-of-bounds addresses triggered fatal exceptions.[3]Modern architectures have transitioned to page-based virtual memory.[2, 6] The MMU translates virtual addresses at the granularity of pages—contiguous blocks of virtual memory mapped to physical page frames.[2, 7] When a CPU generates a virtual address, the MMU splits the address into two distinct logical components: a Virtual Page Number (VPN) and a Page Offset.[2, 5]The Page Offset defines the exact byte boundaries and location within the target page.[2] Because page-based translation maintains identical alignment between virtual and physical pages, the Page Offset is never translated; the MMU routes the offset bits directly to the physical address output bus.[2, 5] The number of offset bits is determined by the page size.[6, 7] For instance, a standard 4\text{ KB} page (2^{12} bytes) requires a 12-bit offset, whereas a 2\text{ MB} huge page (2^{21} bytes) requires a 21-bit offset.[6, 7]Conversely, the VPN represents the page address that must be resolved.[2, 5] The MMU uses the VPN as a lookup key to retrieve the corresponding Physical Page Number (PPN)—also referred to as a Page Frame Number (PFN)—from the active page table hierarchy.[2, 5, 8] Once retrieved, the PPN is combined with the unmodified Page Offset to produce the final physical RAM address.[2, 5]On the software side, virtual memory allocators optimize address space layout by grouping allocations as densely as possible starting from the lowest available levels of the page table.[9] This dense allocation strategy minimizes the spatial overhead of the translation tables by grouping processes into shared memory regions.[9] For example, grouping allocations into the same 2\text{ MB} or 1\text{ GB} virtual regions allows the operating system to map memory without allocating new lower-level page table pages, conserving physical memory.[9]When a translation cannot be resolved—due to either a missing mapping or a protection violation—the MMU halts execution and triggers a page fault exception.[5, 8] The CPU pauses execution and runs a special kernel-space exception handler.[5, 8] The page fault handler determines the cause of the exception: if the target page is swapped out to disk, it pages in the data, updates the page tables, and instructs the CPU to retry the memory instruction.[5, 9] If the fault is due to an invalid access (e.g., trying to write to a read-only page), the kernel terminates the process.[3, 5]
Hierarchical Page Table Trees and ScalabilityA flat linear page table structured as a simple direct-mapped array cannot scale to meet the demands of large virtual address spaces.[3, 9] Because a flat page table must be physically contiguous, its storage footprint grows linearly with the size of the virtual address space, regardless of how much memory is actually allocated.[9] To resolve this scalability issue, modern operating systems implement hierarchical page tables structured as multi-level radix trees.[3, 9, 10] This hierarchical design allows the operating system to leave higher-level page directories unmapped or marked as "not present" if their underlying address ranges are unused.[9]The x86_64 Paging ArchitectureOn x86_64 processors, the default translation model is 4-level paging using 48-bit virtual addresses to map up to 52-bit physical addresses.[6, 10] If supported and enabled, 5-level paging (LA57) adds a fifth level to map 57-bit virtual addresses, providing up to 128\text{ PB} of virtual address space.[6, 10] Both 4-level and 5-level paging require Physical Address Extension (PAE) to be active.[6]Under 4-level paging, the 48-bit virtual address is split into four 9-bit indexes and a 12-bit page offset.[9, 11] Each of the four page table levels—the Page Map Level 4 (PML4), Page Directory Pointer (PDP), Page Directory (PD), and Page Table (PT)—is aligned to a 4\text{ KB} physical memory boundary.[6, 7, 12] Because each table entry is 8 bytes long, a 4\text{ KB} page accommodates exactly 512 entries (512 \times 8\text{ B} = 4096\text{ B}), which aligns with the 9-bit indexing scheme (2^9 = 512).[7, 11]In the Linux kernel, page tables are represented as a five-level hierarchy to provide a uniform abstraction across different hardware architectures.[8, 10] The levels are defined as:Page Global Directory (PGD): The top-level directory.[8, 10] The CR3 register stores the physical address of the active process's PGD.[10, 12]Page Level 4 Directory (P4D): Used to support 5-level paging.[8, 10] On standard 4-level hardware, this level is folded.[8]Page Upper Directory (PUD): Points to the Page Middle Directory.[8]Page Middle Directory (PMD): Points to the Page Table.[8]Page Table Entry (PTE): The lowermost level containing the direct virtual-to-physical mappings.[8]Virtual addresses in 64-bit mode must be in canonical form.[6] For a 48-bit address space, the upper 16 bits (bits 63:48) must be identical to bit 47 (either all 0s or all 1s).[6] For 5-level paging, the upper 7 bits (bits 63:57) must match bit 56.[6] Non-canonical address accesses trigger a general protection fault.[6]Paging Out Page TablesWhile user data pages are frequently paged out to disk, the multi-level radix tree design also allows the operating system to page out the page tables themselves to reclaim physical memory.[9] This optimization is implemented on x86 processors using the Present (P) bit of the Page Directory Entry (PDE).[9]When the operating system decides to swap out a lower-level page table page:It copies the target page table to swap space and clears the Present bit (P=0) in its parent PDE.[9]Because P=0, the hardware page-walk engine ignores the remaining 63 bits of the PDE, allowing the operating system to store custom swap metadata in those bits (such as the disk sector offset).[6, 9]If a process subsequently attempts to access a virtual address that maps through this swapped directory, the hardware page walk fails immediately at the PDE.[9]The CPU triggers a page fault.[9] The kernel's page-fault handler manually traverses the hierarchy, detects that the parent PDE itself is not present, reads the swap metadata from the entry, pages the page table page back into a physical frame, sets the PDE's Present bit to 1, and retries the memory instruction.[9]
Page Table Entry (PTE) Configurations and Flag SemanticsEvery entry in the hierarchical paging structure contains physical address pointers combined with control flags that are interpreted by the hardware MMU to enforce memory protection, cache policies, and page-tracking.[6, 13]P (Present - Bit 0): Specifies whether the page is resident in physical memory.[6] When set, the translation is valid.[6] When cleared, accesses trigger a page fault, allowing the OS to locate the page in swap space.[5, 6]R/W (Read/Write - Bit 1): Controls write permissions.[6] When set, the page is writable; when cleared, it is read-only.[6] The globally active Write-Protect (WP) bit in control register CR0 determines if this restriction applies strictly to user mode or extends to supervisor mode as well.[6] If any parent directory in the hierarchy has R/W=0, the entire subtree is treated as read-only, cascading the write restriction downward.[6]U/S (User/Supervisor - Bit 2): Controls access privilege.[6] When set, the page can be accessed by user-space applications; when cleared, only ring-0 supervisor code can access it.[6, 7] Like write permissions, a user-accessible page requires U/S=1 in both the leaf PTE and all parent directories.[6]PWT (Page Write-Through - Bit 3): Dictates the caching write policy.[6] When set, write-through caching is enabled; when cleared, write-back caching is used.[6]PCD (Page Cache Disable - Bit 4): Controls page caching.[6] If set, caching for the associated page is disabled, which is critical for memory-mapped I/O (MMIO) devices.[6]A (Accessed - Bit 5): Automatically set by the CPU hardware when the page table entry is read during translation.[6] The CPU does not clear this bit, meaning the operating system must clear it manually to track active working sets.[6]D (Dirty - Bit 6): Set by the CPU when a write operation occurs on the mapped physical page.[6] The OS reads this bit to determine if a page has been modified before swapping it out, avoiding redundant disk writes if the page is clean.[6]PS (Page Size - Bit 7): Controls page size.[6] If set in a Page Directory Entry (PDE), it terminates the walk and maps a large page (2\text{ MB} or 4\text{ MB}) directly, bypassing the lower-level Page Table.[6]G (Global - Bit 8): Prevents the entry from being invalidated in the TLB when the CR3 register is reloaded during context switches.[6] CR4.PGE must be set to enable global page support.[6]PAT (Page Attribute Table - Bit 7 in PTE, Bit 12 in 2MB PDE): Works alongside the PCD and PWT bits to select a specific caching memory type from the CPU's Page Attribute Table.[6]PK (Protection Key - Bits 62:59): A 4-bit key that groups virtual pages into protection domains.[6] If CR4.PKE or CR4.PKS is set, the CPU checks the PKRU or PKRS register to enforce user-mode or supervisor-mode access rights.[6]NX / XD (No-Execute / Execute-Disable - Bit 63): Governs instruction execution.[6, 13] If the NXE bit is set in the Extended Feature Enable Register (IA32_EFER.NXE), setting the NX bit on a page table entry blocks execution within that page.[6, 13] This allows the OS to enforce W \wedge X (Write-or-Execute) restrictions, preventing malicious code execution in writable stack or heap memories.[5]
Translation Caching: TLB and Paging-Structure CachesBecause traversing a multi-level page table radix tree for every memory access introduces significant latency, modern microarchitectures utilize high-speed hardware caches.[2, 8, 11, 14]TLB Hit and Miss Performance DynamicsThe primary translation cache is the Translation Lookaside Buffer (TLB), which is a small, associative on-chip cache that stores resolved virtual-to-physical page mappings.[2, 3, 14] When a translation hits in the TLB, the physical address is resolved in approximately one CPU cycle (~0.3 ns).[14] Conversely, a TLB miss forces the MMU's hardware state machine to perform a page table walk.[2, 14] This requires up to four or five serialized memory reads depending on the paging depth, which can cost 50 to 135 cycles on average.[11, 14]Modern processors divide the TLB into a hierarchy of specialized caches.[14, 15] Dedicated first-level instruction TLBs (iTLB) and data TLBs (dTLB) handle high-frequency requests, while a larger, unified second-level TLB (STLB) caches thousands of entries to absorb misses.[14] Despite these hierarchies, translation-heavy workloads with sparse memory footprints—such as large database engines or tiered memory systems—suffer from high TLB pressure.[2, 14, 15]MMU Intermediate CachingTo reduce TLB miss latency, hardware vendors integrate specialized caches within the MMU to store intermediate page table entries.[11, 12, 16] These caches allow the hardware page-walk engine to bypass the upper levels of the radix tree, accelerating address resolution.[11, 12] However, microarchitectures implement this intermediate caching in different ways [12]:Intel processors employ Paging-Structure Caches (PSC).[12, 16] Intel's PSCs are split into level-specific caches (e.g., distinct caches for PML4, PDP, and PD directories).[12] Crucially, they are indexed by portions of the virtual address being translated, functioning as specialized, partial-translation TLBs.[12]AMD processors utilize a unified Page Walk Cache (PWC).[11, 12] AMD's PWC stores page table entries from any directory level in a single, shared cache structure.[12] Unlike Intel's design, the PWC is indexed by the physical address of the cached page table page, making its operation closer to a standard CPU data cache.[12] On ARM architectures, the Translation Control Unit (TCU) implements a dedicated Page Table Walk Cache to cache Stage 1 and Stage 2 translations, reducing nested translation latency in virtualized environments.[17]Further optimization is achieved via spatial prefetching.[16] In a standard 4\text{ KB} page configuration, each 8-byte Page Table Entry (PTE) is aligned within a 64-byte cache line alongside 7 adjacent entries.[7, 16] When the MMU performs a hardware page walk to fetch a requested translation, it reads the entire 64-byte cache line from memory.[16] Sampling-Based Free TLB Prefetching (SBFP) exploits this spatial locality.[16] By prefetching the remaining 7 adjacent PTEs into a prefetch queue "for free" (without generating additional memory transactions), SBFP eliminates up to 37% of the memory accesses caused by page table walks, accelerating translation-heavy workloads.[16]
Multi-Core Synchronization and TLB Shootdown OptimizationsIn symmetric multiprocessing (SMP) systems, multiple CPU cores share a common virtual address space.[18, 19, 20] However, because TLBs are private to each core and are not kept coherent by hardware, changing a page mapping can leave stale entries in remote TLBs.[18, 19] To prevent processes from using stale translations, the operating system must perform a TLB shootdown, forcing remote cores to invalidate their local TLB entries.[14, 18, 20]The Software-Driven IPI Shootdown BottleneckOn x86 architectures, TLB shootdowns are coordinated by the operating system using Inter-Processor Interrupts (IPIs).[14, 18, 19]When a core modifies a shared page table entry:It acts as the initiator, calling flush_tlb_multi() to calculate a target CPU mask (mm_cpumask) of all cores running threads for that process.[14]The initiator sends an IPI to all target cores and enters a spinlock-controlled wait state, stalling its execution.[14, 20, 21]Each target core intercepts the IPI, halts its current thread, executes a local invalidation instruction (such as INVLPG or a CR3 reload), and sends an acknowledgment back to the initiator.[14, 19, 20, 22, 23]The initiator resumes execution only after receiving acknowledgments from all target cores.[14, 20, 21]On many-core NUMA systems, this synchronization overhead scales poorly, crossing NUMA interconnects and taking up to 108\text{ }\mu\text{s}.[14, 24] In virtualized environments, the latency is compounded by "vCPU preemption," where an initiator vCPU stalls while waiting for an acknowledgment from a target vCPU that has been preempted by the hypervisor.[21]Software Shootdown OptimizationsTo mitigate the cost of TLB shootdowns, operating system kernels employ several software optimizations [14, 15]:Batched Flushing (mmu_gather): To avoid executing separate TLB flushes for every unmapped page, the Linux kernel utilizes mmu_gather structures.[14] This per-operation accumulator collects target virtual address ranges and defers the actual TLB flush, delivering a single batched invalidation before releasing the physical pages.[14]Lazy TLB Mode: When a CPU switches from a user-space process to an idle thread or a kernel thread, there are no user mappings to protect.[14] Under Lazy TLB mode, the scheduler sets an is_lazy flag in the CPU's state.[14] The IPI sender skips sending invalidation interrupts to lazy CPUs unless page tables are actively being freed.[14] When the lazy CPU next runs user-space code, it checks the global translation generation (tlb_gen) and executes a full flush if the generation has advanced.[14]TLB Single Page Flush Ceiling: The kernel must balance the precision of the single-page invalidation instruction (INVLPG) against the cost of a full TLB flush (reloading CR3).[15] While INVLPG is precise and preserves unrelated cached translations, executing too many INVLPG instructions is slower than a global flush.[15] In Linux, this behavior is governed by the /sys/kernel/debug/x86/tlb_single_page_flush_ceiling parameter.[15] Once the number of pages to be flushed exceeds this ceiling (typically 33 pages), the kernel switches from individual INVLPG invalidations to a global TLB flush.[14, 15] Lowering this ceiling to 0 disables individual flushes, forcing global flushes exclusively.[15]Architectural Hardware OptimizationsHardware designers have introduced several instruction set extensions to accelerate TLB shootdowns.[14, 18]Process Context Identifiers (PCID)Legacy systems forced a complete TLB flush on every context switch by reloading CR3.[10, 14] Modern architectures bypass this using address space tagging.[14, 22] On x86, the MMU supports PCID, a 12-bit tag in CR3 that identifies up to 4096 unique address spaces.[6, 10, 14] By tagging TLB entries with a PCID, translations for multiple processes can coexist in the TLB simultaneously.[14, 22] During context switches, the kernel loads CR3 with the CR3_NOFLUSH bit (bit 63) set, allowing the incoming process to reuse its cached translations and reducing context switch overhead.[10, 14]Mitigating KPTI OverheadThe discovery of the Meltdown vulnerability required the deployment of Kernel Page Table Isolation (KPTI), which splits each process into two distinct page tables—a "user" table with minimal mappings and a "kernel" table.[10, 14] This isolation originally degraded performance on pre-PCID hardware because every system call required two CR3 writes, each triggering a full TLB flush.[10, 14] PCID made KPTI manageable by dedicating two PCIDs per process—one for user space and one for kernel space.[10, 14]This "doubled" PCID model allows the CPU to switch context between user and kernel modes via CR3 writes without flushing, keeping the TLB warm and dropping KPTI overhead from roughly 30% to 5–10% on modern processors.[10, 14] However, this configuration halves the hardware's available PCID space (which supports up to 4096 PCIDs) to 2048 effective Address Space Identifiers (ASIDs) due to the use of one bit (PTI_CONSUMED_PCID_BITS = 1) to distinguish between user and kernel page tables.[14]Hardware-Broadcast Invalidation (AMD INVLPGB and ARM TLBI)To bypass software-driven IPIs entirely, AMD introduced the INVLPGB (Invalidate Translation Lookaside Buffer Range Broadcast) instruction.[14, 22] When a multi-threaded process is active on four or more CPUs, the Linux kernel assigns it a uniform "global ASID".[14]Instead of sending IPIs, the initiating core executes INVLPGB (coupled with a __tlbsync() instruction), which leverages the system's hardware interconnect to broadcast and execute the invalidation directly in the remote TLBs.[14] This eliminates IPI interrupt latency entirely.[14]Similarly, ARM features the TLBI (TLB Invalidate) instruction combined with a Data Synchronization Barrier (DSB SY).[23, 25] When a core retires a TLBI and the DSB SY instruction completes, hardware guarantees that all remote TLBs have executed the flush.[23] However, ARM's classic broadcast invalidations can be overly broad, flushing Virtually-Indexed Virtually-Tagged (VIVT) L1 caches on victim cores running unrelated tasks, which introduces significant spurious miss penalties.[25]Leased-Based and Asynchronous Coherence (ecoTLB)Academic research explores asynchronous mechanisms like ecoTLB.[20, 24] Rather than letting translations persist indefinitely until explicitly shot down, ecoTLB introduces a lease-based "self-destruct" timer for TLB entries.[20] Upon a TLB miss, the MMU calculates an expiration time for the retrieved entry.[20]If the operating system modifies a page table entry, it can consult a global lease table.[20] If the lease on a translation has already expired, the OS can safely skip the TLB shootdown entirely, as any subsequent access by remote cores will automatically trigger a TLB miss due to the expired lease.[20] For page swapping in disaggregated data centers, asynchronous mechanisms like ecoTLB store versioned PCIDs and update them lazily during context switches, completely removing the synchronous IPI barrier from critical memory paths.[24]
Architectural SynthesisThe evolution of virtual memory architecture is shaped by a constant tension between scaling the virtual address space and minimizing hardware synchronization latency.[1, 11, 14] As microprocessors integrate more cores and memory hierarchies adapt to tiered topologies (such as adjacent HBM combined with CXL memory), the design of translation tables and invalidation protocols must evolve.[1, 14]The transition from flat linear tables to deep hierarchical radix trees solved the physical memory footprint crisis, but increased translation latency by requiring up to five serialized memory accesses for a single TLB miss.[9, 11, 14] Hardware-software co-designs, such as AMD's Page Walk Cache, Intel's Paging-Structure Caches, and spatial prefetching schemes like SBFP, have mitigated this penalty by caching intermediate directories and prefetching adjacent translations "for free".[12, 16]Similarly, the multi-core scaling bottleneck has forced a transition away from software-driven, IPI-bound TLB shootdowns.[14, 18] On massive multi-socket systems, IPI latency and spinlock contention degrade performance during operations like memory unmapping or swapping.[14, 20, 24]To sustain throughput, modern hardware architectures increasingly rely on hardware-enforced broadcast mechanisms (like AMD's INVLPGB and ARM's TLBI) or explore academic lease-based approaches (like ecoTLB).[14, 20] These innovations demonstrate that as system scale increases, virtual memory translation must move toward precise, hardware-supported, and asynchronous coherence models to prevent address translation from bottlenecking parallel computing performance.[14, 20, 24]
